//This loads the IFrame Player API code asynchronously.
    var tag = document.createElement('script');
    tag.src = "https://www.youtube.com/iframe_api";
    var firstScriptTag = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
    firstScriptTag.parentNode.insertBefore(tag, firstScriptTag);

    // This function creates an <iframe> (and YouTube player) after the API code downloads. VIDEO 1
    var player;
    function onYouTubeIframeAPIReady() {
        if (typeof(YT) !== 'undefined' && typeof(YT.Player) !== 'undefined') {
            player = new YT.Player('youTubePlayer', {
                videoId: 'qM1BYkEA17A', 
               playerVars: { 'autoplay': 1 },
               events: {
                   'onReady': onPlayerReady,
                   'onPlaybackQualityChange': onPlayerPlaybackQualityChange,
                   'onStateChange': onPlayerStateChange
            }
            });
        }
    }

function onPlayerPlaybackQualityChange(event) {
    var playbackQuality = event.target.getPlaybackQuality();
    var suggestedQuality = 'hd720';

    console.log("Quality changed to: " + playbackQuality );

    if( playbackQuality !== 'hd720') {
        console.log("Setting quality to " + suggestedQuality );
        event.target.setPlaybackQuality( suggestedQuality );
    }
}

    function onPlayerReady(event) {
        //we aren't going to do anything here
        //event.target.playVideo();
        player.setPlaybackQuality('hd720');
    }

    function onPlayerStateChange(event) {
        // track when user clicks to Play
        if (event.data == YT.PlayerState.PLAYING) {
             var rate = 'hd720';
              player.setPlaybackQuality(rate);
             console.log('playing');
         
        }

        // track when user clicks to Pause
        if (event.data == YT.PlayerState.PAUSED) {
             console.log('paused');
        }

        // track when video ends
        if (event.data == YT.PlayerState.ENDED) {
           console.log('ended');
            document.getElementById('youTubePlayer').style.display='none';
             document.getElementById("yt-tracker").src="https://bs.serving-sys.com/serving/adServer.bs?cn=display&c=19&mc=imp&pli=23659909&PluID=0&ord=%%CACHEBUSTER%%&rtu=-1";
        }
    }

    var player2;
    function onYouTubeIframeAPIReady2() {
        if (typeof(YT) !== 'undefined' && typeof(YT.Player) !== 'undefined') {
            player2 = new YT.Player('youTubePlayer2', {
                videoId: 'A0ZP1Rour8k', 
                playerVars: { 'autoplay': 1 },
                events: {
                   'onReady': onPlayerReady2,
                   'onPlaybackQualityChange': onPlayerPlaybackQualityChange2,
                   'onStateChange': onPlayerStateChange2
                }
            });
        }
    }

    function onPlayerReady2(event2) {
        event2.target.playVideo();
    }

 function onPlayerPlaybackQualityChange2(event2) {
    var playbackQuality = event2.target.getPlaybackQuality();
    var suggestedQuality = 'hd720';

    console.log("Quality changed to: " + playbackQuality );

}

           console.log('ended');
            document.getElementById('youTubePlayer2').style.display='none';
        }
    }

setTimeout(function(){ onYouTubeIframeAPIReady2()  }, 1000);

class="ArticleHeader_headline">RPT-COLUMN-Friend or foe? Canadian imports split U.S. aluminium sector: Andy Home</h1></div></div><div class="TwoColumnLayout_column TwoColumnLayout_right"></div></div></div></div><div class="lower-container"><div class="TwoColumnLayout_container StandardArticle_content TwoColumnLayout_fluid-left"><div class="TwoColumnLayout_column TwoColumnLayout_left"><div class="StandardArticleBody_container"><div class="StandardArticleBody_body"><p>(Repeats June 19 story with no changes to text) </p><p>* The opinions expressed here are those of the author, a columnist for Reuters. </p><p>* US Midwest Aluminium Premium: <a href="https://tmsnrt.rs/3fGM2OC">tmsnrt.rs/3fGM2OC</a> </p><p>* US Imports of Canadian Aluminium: <a href="https://tmsnrt.rs/2YTWhbE">tmsnrt.rs/2YTWhbE</a> </p><p>By Andy Home </p><p>LONDON, June 19 (Reuters) - A surge in Canadian aluminium imports “is destroying what remains of the United States industry”. </p><p>The dramatic warning comes from the American Primary Aluminum Association (APAA), which represents Century Aluminum and Magnitude 7 Metals, two of the last three remaining primary producers in the United States. </p><p>The solution, they argue in a May 27 letter to U.S. Trade Representative Robert Lighthizer and Commerce Secretary Wilbur Ross, is to revoke Canada’s exemption from import tariffs. </p><p>The U.S. Aluminum Association (AA), which has a much larger membership including the country’s third producer Alcoa, vehemently disagrees. </p><p>“The Great Canadian Aluminum Distraction” has diverted attention from the sector’s more pressing issues with Chinese overcapacity and exports, it claims. </p><p>This uncivil war of words has fueled a sharp turnaround in the Midwest aluminium premium as the regional market reassesses the prospect of renewed tariffs on the largest-volume supplier to the U.S. market. </p><p>It also lays bare the failure of President Donald Trump’s 10% tariffs to revitalise domestic production of a metal the administration deems critical to national security. </p><p>But is Canada to blame? Or is it the solution? </p><h3>BATTLE OF THE SURGE </h3><p>The APAA claims that Canadian imports of primary aluminium have surged since the country was exempted from tariffs in May 2019. </p><p>The word has lobbying resonance. </p><p>The trade agreement between the two countries includes a provision that tariffs on Canadian aluminium can be re-imposed if imports “surge meaningfully beyond historic volumes of trade over a period of time, with consideration of market share”. </p><div></div><p>Unfortunately, the trade negotiators didn’t specify what would represent a “meaningful” surge or what time period would be used as reference point. </p><p>A diplomatic oversight that now forms a statistical battleground between the APAA and the AA. </p><p>It is true that more Canadian aluminium has been entering the United States this year. </p><p>Imports of primary metal jumped by 46% year-on-year in the first quarter of 2020, according to research house CRU. </p><p>But there are some important caveats. </p><p>Firstly, Canadian production has been rising thanks to the return of the Becancour smelter after a bitter two-year strike that ended in July 2019. The plant produced 72,000 tonnes in January-March, compared with just 77,000 tonnes over the whole of 2019. </p><p>Secondly, there is the not so little matter of COVID-19 and the demand destruction caused by the lockdown of North America’s automotive and construction sectors. </p><p>There is a lot of surplus aluminium around at the moment. London Metal Exchange (LME) warehouse stocks have risen from under a million tonnes in the middle of March to 1.6 million. </p><p>Much of that surplus, moreover, is in the form of commodity-grade metal. In response to collapsing automotive demand aluminium producers have shifted their mix away from value-added products to so-called “P1020” aluminium that can be delivered to LME warehouses. </p><p>It’s precisely that form of metal that the APAA claims has been surging across the border. </p><p>Imports of Canadian alloyed metal, on the other hand, are on course to fall 5% this year as the product mix changes, CRU estimates. </p><p>Disentangling such market dynamics from tariff dynamics is highly problematic. </p><p>And without an agreed statistical anchor point, one side’s surge is another’s mean reversion. </p><p>The first quarter imports were actually only 3% higher than the first-quarter average in 2015-2017, notes CRU. </p><p>Canada’s share of the import mix in the quarter was 67%, exactly the same as the 30-year average, according to the AA. </p><div></div><h3>FRIEND OR FOE? </h3><p>There is one underlying truth in this statistical maze. </p><p>Any post-tariff lift to U.S. aluminium production has run its course with all three operators under financial stress at current prices. </p><p>Alcoa is shutting its Ferndale smelter in the state of Washington, reducing the number of operating plants to just six. </p><p>Ferndale is an old plant, first commissioned in 1966. Production lines have been curtailed and reactivated during past aluminium price cycles. </p><p>Indeed, all the remaining U.S. smelters are old, leaving them in the cost-curve danger zone whenever the price falls. </p><p>Century’s Hawesville, Sebree and Mt Holley smelters date from 1969, 1973 and 1980 respectively. </p><p>The New Madrid smelter bought out of bankruptcy by Magnitude 7 metals was built in 1971. Its reactivation was hailed by the Trump administration as a tariffs success story but local residents are now living with the dirtiest air in the country. </p><p>Tariffs can at best only partially shield such operators from global price trends, even while penalising downstream U.S. aluminium users. </p><p>That’s a problem for the United States given the determination to build secure supply chains for critical metals such as aluminium. </p><p>Particularly since the world’s largest exporter of primary metal is Russia and the largest exporter of semi-manufactured products is China, two highly problematic trade partners. </p><p>If the United States is serious about its long-term aluminium supply, it will need to either form an alliance with a friendly producer country or directly subsidise new capacity. </p><p>The latter may seem an outlandish idea but the Department of Defense has already been authorised to invest directly into other vulnerable critical mineral supply chains such as rare earths. </p><p>In either case Canada is the obvious aluminium choice. </p><p>Its smelters are newer, lower-cost and greener thanks to Quebec’s hydro-power systems. It may have room for at least another one. It’s far from clear if the more fractured U.S. energy market can accommodate a new power-hungry aluminium smelter. </p><p>Canada’s aluminium sector has also historically been considered an integral part of the United States’ defence sector, a fact easily forgotten in the fractious trade relationship of the last two years. </p><p>Re-imposing tariffs on Canadian aluminium may have short-term political appeal ahead of U.S. elections later this year but the problem of the United States’ ageing smelters isn’t going to go away. </p><p>Tariffs would actively work against the obvious long-term solution to the United States’ problem with primary aluminium. </p><div></div><p>It’s ironic that Canadian imports are roiling the aluminium sector even as the two countries work towards a broader metallic alliance. </p><p>The U.S.-Canada Critical Minerals Working Group held its second virtual meeting on June 17 with both sides reaffirming their “commitment to further strengthen the U.S.-Canada supply chain for critical minerals that are essential to our mutual security and future prosperity”. </p><p>We don’t know if aluminium was on the agenda. If not, it should have been. </p><div class="Attribution_container"><div class="Attribution_attribution"><p class="Attribution_content">Editing by Emelia Sithole-Matarise</p></div></div><div News - Americas","path":"/news/archive/americasRegulatoryNews"},"headline":"RPT-COLUMN-Friend or foe? Canadian imports split U.S. aluminium sector: Andy Home","authors":[{"name":"Andy Home","journalist_url":"/journalists/andy-home"}],"shareUrl":"https://reut.rs/2AOtddX","published":1592787656,"updated":0,"path":"/article/us-aluminium-ahome/rpt-column-friend-or-foe-canadian-imports-split-u-s-aluminium-sector-andy-home-idUSL8N2DW3O2","body":"\u003cp\u003e(Repeats June 19 story with no changes to text)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e* The opinions expressed here are those of the author, a\ncolumnist\nfor Reuters.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e* US Midwest Aluminium Premium: \u003ca href\u003d\"https://tmsnrt.rs/3fGM2OC\"\u003etmsnrt.rs/3fGM2OC\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e* US Imports of Canadian Aluminium: \u003ca href\u003d\"https://tmsnrt.rs/2YTWhbE\"\u003etmsnrt.rs/2YTWhbE\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBy Andy Home\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLONDON, June 19 (Reuters) - A surge in Canadian aluminium\nimports \"is destroying what remains of the United States\nindustry\".\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe dramatic warning comes from the American Primary\nAluminum Association (APAA), which represents Century Aluminum\nand Magnitude 7 Metals, two of the last three remaining\nprimary producers in the United States.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe solution, they argue in a May 27 letter to U.S. Trade\nRepresentative Robert Lighthizer and Commerce Secretary Wilbur\nRoss, is to revoke Canada\u0027s exemption from import tariffs.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe U.S. Aluminum Association (AA), which has a much larger\nmembership including the country\u0027s third producer Alcoa,\nvehemently disagrees.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\"The Great Canadian Aluminum Distraction\" has diverted\nattention from the sector\u0027s more pressing issues with Chinese\novercapacity and exports, it claims.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis uncivil war of words has fueled a sharp turnaround in\nthe Midwest aluminium premium as the regional market reassesses\nthe prospect of renewed tariffs on the largest-volume supplier\nto the U.S. market.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt also lays bare the failure of President Donald Trump\u0027s\n10% tariffs to revitalise domestic production of a metal the\nadministration deems critical to national security.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut is Canada to blame? Or is it the solution?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBATTLE OF THE SURGE\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe APAA claims that Canadian imports of primary aluminium\nhave surged since the country was exempted from tariffs in May\n2019.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe word has lobbying resonance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe trade agreement between the two countries includes a\nprovision that tariffs on Canadian aluminium can be re-imposed\nif imports \"surge meaningfully beyond historic volumes of trade\nover a period of time, with consideration of market share\".\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUnfortunately, the trade negotiators didn\u0027t specify what\nwould represent a \"meaningful\" surge or what time period would\nbe used as reference point.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA diplomatic oversight that now forms a statistical\nbattleground between the APAA and the AA.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt is true that more Canadian aluminium has been entering\nthe United States this year.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eImports of primary metal jumped by 46% year-on-year in the\nfirst quarter of 2020, according to research house CRU.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBut there are some important caveats.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFirstly, Canadian production has been rising thanks to the\nreturn of the Becancour smelter after a bitter two-year strike\nthat ended in July 2019. The plant produced 72,000 tonnes in\nJanuary-March, compared with just 77,000 tonnes over the whole\nof 2019.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eSecondly, there is the not so little matter of COVID-19 and\nthe demand destruction caused by the lockdown of North America\u0027s\nautomotive and construction sectors.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is a lot of surplus aluminium around at the moment.\nLondon Metal Exchange (LME) warehouse stocks have risen from\nunder a million tonnes in the middle of March to 1.6 million.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMuch of that surplus, moreover, is in the form of\ncommodity-grade metal. In response to collapsing automotive\ndemand aluminium producers have shifted their mix away from\nvalue-added products to so-called \"P1020\" aluminium that can be\ndelivered to LME warehouses.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt\u0027s precisely that form of metal that the APAA claims has\nbeen surging across the border.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eImports of Canadian alloyed metal, on the other hand, are on\ncourse to fall 5% this year as the product mix changes, CRU\nestimates.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDisentangling such market dynamics from tariff dynamics is\nhighly problematic.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAnd without an agreed statistical anchor point, one side\u0027s\nsurge is another\u0027s mean reversion.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe first quarter imports were actually only 3% higher than\nthe first-quarter average in 2015-2017, notes CRU.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCanada\u0027s share of the import mix in the quarter was 67%,\nexactly the same as the 30-year average, according to the AA.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFRIEND OR FOE?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThere is one underlying truth in this statistical maze.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAny post-tariff lift to U.S. aluminium production has run\nits course with all three operators under financial stress at\ncurrent prices.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAlcoa is shutting its Ferndale smelter in the state of\nWashington, reducing the number of operating plants to just six.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFerndale is an old plant, first commissioned in 1966.\nProduction lines have been curtailed and reactivated during past\naluminium price cycles.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIndeed, all the remaining U.S. smelters are old, leaving\nthem in the cost-curve danger zone whenever the price falls.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCentury\u0027s Hawesville, Sebree and Mt Holley smelters date\nfrom 1969, 1973 and 1980 respectively.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe New Madrid smelter bought out of bankruptcy by Magnitude\n7 metals was built in 1971. Its reactivation was hailed by the\nTrump administration as a tariffs success story but local\nresidents are now living with the dirtiest air in the country.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTariffs can at best only partially shield such operators\nfrom global price trends, even while penalising downstream U.S.\naluminium users.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThat\u0027s a problem for the United States given the\ndetermination to build secure supply chains for critical metals\nsuch as aluminium.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eParticularly since the world\u0027s largest exporter of primary\nmetal is Russia and the largest exporter of semi-manufactured\nproducts is China, two highly problematic trade partners.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIf the United States is serious about its long-term\naluminium supply, it will need to either form an alliance with a\nfriendly producer country or directly subsidise new capacity.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe latter may seem an outlandish idea but the Department of\nDefense has already been authorised to invest directly into\nother vulnerable critical mineral supply chains such as rare\nearths.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn either case Canada is the obvious aluminium choice.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIts smelters are newer, lower-cost and greener thanks to\nQuebec\u0027s hydro-power systems. It may have room for at least\nanother one. It\u0027s far from clear if the more fractured U.S.\nenergy market can accommodate a new power-hungry aluminium\nsmelter.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCanada\u0027s aluminium sector has also historically been\nconsidered an integral part of the United States\u0027 defence\nsector, a fact easily forgotten in the fractious trade\nrelationship of the last two years.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eRe-imposing tariffs on Canadian aluminium may have\nshort-term political appeal ahead of U.S. elections later this\nyear but the problem of the United States\u0027 ageing smelters isn\u0027t\ngoing to go away.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTariffs would actively work against the obvious long-term\nsolution to the United States\u0027 problem with primary aluminium.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIt\u0027s ironic that Canadian imports are roiling the aluminium\nsector even as the two countries work towards a broader metallic\nalliance.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe U.S.-Canada Critical Minerals Working Group held its\nsecond virtual meeting on June 17 with both sides reaffirming\ntheir \"commitment to further strengthen the U.S.-Canada supply\nchain for critical minerals that are essential to our mutual\nsecurity and future prosperity\".\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWe don\u0027t know if aluminium was on the agenda.
